        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       On July 1994,
       <a href="/wiki/Comet_Shoemaker%E2%80%93Levy_9" title="Comet Shoemaker–Levy 9">
        Comet Shoemaker–Levy 9
       </a>
       was a
       <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">
        comet
       </a>
       that broke apart and collided with
       <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">
        Jupiter
       </a>
       , providing the first direct observation of an extraterrestrial collision of
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">
        Solar System
       </a>
       objects.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA2005_70-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA2005-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The event served as a "wake-up call", and astronomers responded by starting programs such as
       <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Near-Earth_Asteroid_Research" title="Lincoln Near-Earth Asteroid Research">
        Lincoln Near-Earth Asteroid Research
       </a>
       (LINEAR),
       <a href="/wiki/Near-Earth_Asteroid_Tracking" title="Near-Earth Asteroid Tracking">
        Near-Earth Asteroid Tracking
       </a>
       (NEAT),
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lowell_Observatory_Near-Earth_Object_Search" title="Lowell Observatory Near-Earth Object Search">
        Lowell Observatory Near-Earth Object Search
       </a>
       (LONEOS) and several others which have drastically increased the rate of asteroid discovery.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jupiter_2009_impact_event" title="Jupiter 2009 impact event">
        2009 Jupiter impact event
       </a>
       happened on July 19 when a new black spot about the size of Earth was discovered in Jupiter's southern hemisphere by
       <a href="/wiki/Amateur_astronomy" title="Amateur astronomy">
        amateur astronomer
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Wesley" title="Anthony Wesley">
        Anthony Wesley
       </a>
       . Thermal infrared analysis showed it was warm and spectroscopic methods detected ammonia.
       <a href="/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory" title="Jet Propulsion Laboratory">
        JPL
       </a>
       scientists confirmed that there was another impact event on Jupiter, probably involving a small undiscovered comet or other icy body.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
        <a href="#cite_note-72">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
        <a href="#cite_note-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The impactor is estimated to have been about 200–500 meters in diameter.
      </p>
      <p>
       A
       <a href="/wiki/2010_Jupiter_impact_event" title="2010 Jupiter impact event">
        2010 Jupiter impact event
       </a>
       occurred on June 3 involving an object estimated at 8–13 meters was recorded and first reported by Anthony Wesley.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sayanagi_74-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-sayanagi-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bakich_75-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-bakich-75">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On Sept 10, 2012, amateur astronomer Dan Petersen visually detected a fireball on Jupiter that lasted 1 or 2 seconds using a 12″
       <a href="/wiki/Meade_LX200" title="Meade LX200">
        LX200
       </a>
       . It was estimated that the fireball was created by a meteoroid less than 10 meters in diameter.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-impact2012_77-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-impact2012-77">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On March 17, 2016, a Jupiter impact event occurred involving an unknown object, possibly a small comet or asteroid estimated at 30–90 meters (or a few hundred feet) across. Footage of the event was recorded from the telescope of amateur astronomer John McKeon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_impacts">
        Other impacts
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Impact_event&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Other impacts">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Asteroid_Collision_Hubble.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2965" data-file-width="1706" height="382" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Asteroid_Collision_Hubble.jpg/220px-Asteroid_Collision_Hubble.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Asteroid_Collision_Hubble.jpg/330px-Asteroid_Collision_Hubble.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Asteroid_Collision_Hubble.jpg/440px-Asteroid_Collision_Hubble.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Asteroid_Collision_Hubble.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">
          Hubble
         </a>
         's
         <a href="/wiki/Wide_Field_Camera_3" title="Wide Field Camera 3">
          Wide Field Camera 3
         </a>
         clearly shows the slow evolution of the debris coming from asteroid
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P/2010_A2" title="P/2010 A2">
          P/2010 A2
         </a>
         , assumed to be due to a collision with a smaller asteroid.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In 1998, two comets were observed plunging toward the
       <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
        Sun
       </a>
       in close succession. The first of these was on June 1 and the second the next day. A video of this, followed by a dramatic ejection of solar gas (unrelated to the impacts), can be found at the NASA
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
        <a href="#cite_note-79">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       website. Both of these comets evaporated before coming into contact with the surface of the Sun. According to a theory by NASA
       <a href="/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory" title="Jet Propulsion Laboratory">
        Jet Propulsion Laboratory
       </a>
       scientist
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zden%C4%9Bk_Sekanina" title="Zdeněk Sekanina">
        Zdeněk Sekanina
       </a>
       , the latest impactor to actually make contact with the Sun was the "supercomet"
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comet_Howard-Koomen-Michels" title="Comet Howard-Koomen-Michels">
        Howard-Koomen-Michels
       </a>
       on August 30, 1979.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Self-published_sources" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
          <span title="The material near this tag may rely on a self-published source. (February 2017)">
           self-published source?
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       (See also
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sungrazer" title="Sungrazer">
        sungrazer
       </a>
       .)
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2010, between January and May,
       <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">
        Hubble
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/Wide_Field_Camera_3" title="Wide Field Camera 3">
        Wide Field Camera 3
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
        <a href="#cite_note-81">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
       took images of an unusual X shape originated in the aftermath of the collision between asteroid
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P/2010_A2" title="P/2010 A2">
        P/2010 A2
       </a>
       with a smaller
       <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">
        asteroid
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Around March 27, 2012, based on evidence, there were signs of an impact on
       <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">
        Mars
       </a>
       . Images from the
       <a href="/wiki/Mars_Reconnaissance_Orbiter" title="Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter">
        Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter
       </a>
       provide compelling evidence of the largest impact observed to date on Mars in the form of fresh craters, the largest measuring 48.5 by 43.5 meters. It is estimated to be caused by an impactor 3 to 5 meters long.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On March 19, 2013, an impact occurred on the
       <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
        Moon
       </a>
       that was visible from Earth, when a boulder-sized 30 cm meteoroid slammed into the lunar surface at 90,000 km/h (56,000 mph) creating a 20-meter crater.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
        <a href="#cite_note-84">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
       NASA has actively monitored lunar impacts since 2005,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
        <a href="#cite_note-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
       tracking hundreds of candidate events.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
        <a href="#cite_note-86">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Extrasolar_impacts">
        Extrasolar impacts
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Impact_event&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Extrasolar impacts">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA18469-AsteroidCollision-NearStarNGC2547-ID8-2013.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2253" data-file-width="4005" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/PIA18469-AsteroidCollision-NearStarNGC2547-ID8-2013.jpg/220px-PIA18469-AsteroidCollision-NearStarNGC2547-ID8-2013.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/PIA18469-AsteroidCollision-NearStarNGC2547-ID8-2013.jpg/330px-PIA18469-AsteroidCollision-NearStarNGC2547-ID8-2013.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/PIA18469-AsteroidCollision-NearStarNGC2547-ID8-2013.jpg/440px-PIA18469-AsteroidCollision-NearStarNGC2547-ID8-2013.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA18469-AsteroidCollision-NearStarNGC2547-ID8-2013.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Asteroid collision led to the building of planets near star
         <a href="/wiki/NGC_2547" title="NGC 2547">
          NGC 2547
         </a>
         -ID8 (artist concept).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Collisions between galaxies, or
       <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_merger" title="Galaxy merger">
        galaxy mergers
       </a>
       , have been observed directly by space telescopes such as Hubble and Spitzer. However, collisions in planetary systems including
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stellar_collisions" title="Stellar collisions">
        stellar collisions
       </a>
       , while long speculated, have only recently begun to be observed directly.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2013, an impact between minor planets was detected around the star
       <a href="/wiki/NGC_2547" title="NGC 2547">
        NGC 2547
       </a>
       by Spitzer and confirmed by ground observations. Computer modelling suggests that the impact involved large asteroids or
       <a href="/wiki/Protoplanet" title="Protoplanet">
        protoplanets
       </a>
       similar to the events believed to have led to the formation of terrestrial planets like the Earth.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space.com_4-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-space.com-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Popular_culture">
        Popular culture
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Impact_event&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Popular culture">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Science_fiction_novels">
        Science fiction novels
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Impact_event&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Science fiction novels">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Asteroids_in_fiction#Collisions_with_planets" title="Asteroids in fiction">
        Asteroids in fiction § Collisions with planets
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Numerous science fiction stories and novels center around an impact event. One of the first and more popular is
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Off_on_a_Comet" title="Off on a Comet">
         Off on a Comet
        </a>
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="fr">
         Hector Servadac
        </span>
       </i>
       ) by
       <a href="/wiki/Jules_Verne" title="Jules Verne">
        Jules Verne
       </a>
       , published in 1877, and
       <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells" title="H. G. Wells">
        H. G. Wells
       </a>
       wrote about such an event in his 1897 short story "
       <a href="/wiki/The_Star_(Wells_short_story)" title="The Star (Wells short story)">
        The Star
       </a>
       ." In more modern times, possibly the best-selling was the novel
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Lucifer%27s_Hammer" title="Lucifer's Hammer">
         Lucifer's Hammer
        </a>
       </i>
       by
       <a href="/wiki/Larry_Niven" title="Larry Niven">
        Larry Niven
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Pournelle" title="Jerry Pournelle">
        Jerry Pournelle
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Arthur_C._Clarke" title="Arthur C. Clarke">
        Arthur C. Clarke
       </a>
       's novel
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Rendezvous_with_Rama" title="Rendezvous with Rama">
         Rendezvous with Rama
        </a>
       </i>
       opens with a significant asteroid impact in northern Italy in the year 2077 which gives rise to the Spaceguard Project, which later discovers the Rama spacecraft. In 1992 a Congressional study in the U.S. led to NASA being directed to undertake the "
       <a href="/wiki/Spaceguard" title="Spaceguard">
        Spaceguard
       </a>
       Survey", with the novel being named as the inspiration for the name to search for Earth-impacting asteroids.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
        <a href="#cite_note-87">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This in turn inspired Clarke's 1993 novel
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Hammer_of_God_(Clarke_novel)" title="The Hammer of God (Clarke novel)">
         The Hammer of God
        </a>
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       A variation on the traditional impact story was provided by
       <a href="/wiki/Jack_McDevitt" title="Jack McDevitt">
        Jack McDevitt
       </a>
       's 1999 novel
       <i>
        Moonfall
       </i>
       , in which a very large comet traveling at interstellar velocities collides with and partially destroys the Moon, fragments of which then collide with the Earth. The 1985 Niven and Pournelle novel
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Footfall" title="Footfall">
         Footfall
        </a>
       </i>
       features the examination of the effects of planetary warfare conducted by an alien species that culminates in the use of asteroids to bombard the planet, creating very large craters and the human species' near-extinction.
       <a href="/wiki/Robert_A._Heinlein" title="Robert A. Heinlein">
        Robert A. Heinlein
       </a>
       used the concept of guided meteors in his novel
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Moon_is_a_Harsh_Mistress" title="The Moon is a Harsh Mistress">
         The Moon is a Harsh Mistress
        </a>
       </i>
       , in which Moon rebels use rock-filled shipping containers as a weapon against their Earth oppressors.
      </p>
      <p>
       Some science fiction has concerned itself not with the specifics of the impact event and/or its prevention or avoidance but its secondary effects on human society.
       <a href="/wiki/Ben_H._Winters" title="Ben H. Winters">
        Ben H. Winters
       </a>
       ' 2012 novel
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Last_Policeman" title="The Last Policeman">
         The Last Policeman
        </a>
       </i>
       is set six months prior to an asteroid collision, following a murder investigation that is complicated by the political and cultural responses to the impending event.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cinema_and_television">
        Cinema and television
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Impact_event&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Cinema and television">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_impact_avoidance#Fictional_representations" title="Asteroid impact avoidance">
        Asteroid impact avoidance § Fictional representations
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Several
       <a href="/wiki/Disaster_film" title="Disaster film">
        disaster films
       </a>
       center on actual or threatened impact events. Released during the turbulence of
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">
        World War I
       </a>
       , the Danish feature film
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_End_of_the_World_(1916_film)" title="The End of the World (1916 film)">
         The End of the World
        </a>
       </i>
       revolves around the near-miss of a comet which causes fire showers and social unrest in Europe.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
        <a href="#cite_note-88">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/When_Worlds_Collide_(1951_film)" title="When Worlds Collide (1951 film)">
         When Worlds Collide
        </a>
       </i>
       (1951), based on a 1933 novel by
       <a href="/wiki/Philip_Wylie" title="Philip Wylie">
        Philip Wylie
       </a>
       , deals with two planets on a collision course with Earth—the smaller planet a "near miss," causing extensive damage and destruction, followed by a direct hit from the larger planet.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
        <a href="#cite_note-89">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Meteor_(film)" title="Meteor (film)">
         Meteor
        </a>
       </i>
       (1979) features small asteroid fragments and a large 8 km (5 mi)-wide asteroid heading for Earth after being wrenched out of orbit by a collision with a comet. Orbiting U.S. and Soviet nuclear weapons platforms are turned away from their respective earthbound targets and toward the incoming threat. The concept for this defense mechanism was taken from a 1968
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MIT" title="MIT">
        MIT
       </a>
       study called "
       <a href="/wiki/1566_Icarus#Project_Icarus" title="1566 Icarus">
        Project Icarus
       </a>
       ."
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1998, two films were released in the United States on the subject of attempting to stop impact events: Paramount/DreamWorks'
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Deep_Impact_(film)" title="Deep Impact (film)">
         Deep Impact
        </a>
       </i>
       , about a comet, and Touchstone Pictures'
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Armageddon_(1998_film)" title="Armageddon (1998 film)">
         Armageddon
        </a>
       </i>
       , about an asteroid. Both involved using
       <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">
        Space Shuttle
       </a>
       -derived craft to deliver
       <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">
        nuclear weapons
       </a>
       to destroy their targets. The 2008 American Broadcasting Company's
       <a href="/wiki/Miniseries" title="Miniseries">
        miniseries
       </a>
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Impact_(TV_miniseries)" title="Impact (TV miniseries)">
         Impact
        </a>
       </i>
       deals with a splinter of a
       <a href="/wiki/Brown_dwarf" title="Brown dwarf">
        brown dwarf
       </a>
       hidden in a meteor shower which strikes the Moon and sends it on a collision course with Earth. The 2011 film
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Melancholia_(2011_film)" title="Melancholia (2011 film)">
         Melancholia
        </a>
       </i>
       uses the motif of an impact event incorporated in the aesthetics of
       <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">
        Romanticism
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
        <a href="#cite_note-90">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
